Jean-Baptiste replaced the receiver. His hand was steady. He had done it. He had used the machine’s own obsession with order to forge a passport to its outer wall. He had a reason to be on the N20. He had a name for the driver. Gérard.

He left the booth. The street outside seemed brighter, sharper, more dangerous. He had a destination. He had a cover. He had, nestled in the heart of his official task, his true mission: to find the grain in the wood. To learn something, anything, that could be used as a lever.

He went to a garage he knew, a filthy establishment run by a one-legged veteran of Verdun who rented out ancient, coughing vehicles by the hour. He paid for a dented Renault Juvaquatre, its engine sounding like a spoon in a tin of nuts. It was inconspicuous. It was the car of a minor functionary on a dull errand.

He drove south, following the signs for Porte d’Orléans. The city unspooled around him, grand boulevards giving way to narrower streets, then to the zone, the shantytowns and rubbish heaps that clung to the old fortifications. Then he was on the route nationale, a strip of battered asphalt running between fields still scarred with old trenches and gun emplacements.

He drove past the turn for Châtillon. He was looking for the café. He saw it a kilometre further on: a low, long building of yellowing stucco, with a flat roof and a cracked sign showing a winged wheel and the words LE RELAIS DE LA LIBERTÉ. A few heavy lorries were parked in the gravel lot beside it.

He pulled in, parked the Renault away from the trucks. He sat for a moment, watching. The place exhaled a breath of fried food and diesel. Men in heavy jackets came and went from the door. This was the place. The thread.

But he could not go in now. He had an appointment at a gate. He had to be Clerk Barrau, first. The conspirator would have to wait.

He put the car in gear and turned back towards Châtillon.

The old factory was not hidden. It sprawled at the end of a long, straight road lined with skeletal poplars. It was a monument to a failed modernity: long, low buildings of reinforced concrete, their windows mostly boarded over. A high fence, new, topped with razor wire, encircled the complex. A single gate, wide enough for trucks, was set into it. A guardhouse stood beside it, smoke trickling from a chimney pipe.

As he approached, he saw the black van. It was parked just inside the gate. Two figures in drab overalls leaned against it, smoking. Drivers, waiting.

He stopped the Renault at the gate. A guard emerged from the hut, unsmiling, a rifle slung over his shoulder.

“Jean-Baptiste Barrau, Bureau of National Statistics. Here to see the driver, Gérard. Regarding a documentation discrepancy for the incoming asset.”

The guard looked him over, checked a clipboard. He gave a curt nod, went to the van, and spoke to one of the men. The taller of the two straightened, threw his cigarette down, and trudged to the gate. He was a man in his fifties, with a face like a well-worn saddle, and eyes that had seen too many kilometres of empty road.

“You the clerk?” he grunted.

“Barrau. There’s an issue with the ancillary inventory. I need to see the manifest you have, and the materials themselves.”

Gérard sighed, the sigh of a man perpetually besieged by idiots in offices. “It’s all in the cab. And the ‘materials’ are a sack of rags and a food bowl. That’s it.”

“Protocol requires a visual check. I’ll be quick.”

Gérard led him to the cab of the van. The other driver watched, bored. The interior smelled of tobacco and old sweat. Gérard pulled a folded sheet of paper from a compartment and handed it over. It was a standard transport manifest. At the bottom, under “Ancillary Items,” was scrawled: One (1) burlap sack, contents: assorted cloth. One (1) enamel bowl.

“And the items?” Jean-Baptiste asked, his pen poised over a fresh sheet of his own paper.

Gérard jerked a thumb towards the back of the van. “In there. With him.”

Jean-Baptiste’s heart stuttered. “The asset is still in the van?”

“They’re backed up inside. Told us to wait. He’s sedated. Won’t wake up for hours.” Gérard lit another cigarette. “You wanna see the sack, you gotta look through the hatch. I’m not opening the doors. Regulations.”

This was it. A chance. Not to free, not to communicate, but to witness. To confirm the asset was here, alive, in this moment.

“I need to verify,” Jean-Baptiste said, his voice dry.

Gérard shrugged. He led him to the rear doors. There was a small, reinforced viewing hatch at eye level. He slid back a metal plate, revealing a thick pane of glass.

“Make it fast.”

Jean-Baptiste leaned in.

The interior of the van was dark, but a shaft of grey light from the hatch fell across the space. He saw the brute immediately. It was lying on its side on a bare metal floor, secured by thick straps across its chest and thighs. Its eyes were closed. Its massive chest rose and fell slowly, a machine in low gear. The sedation was profound. It did not look peaceful; it looked empty. A vessel poured out.

Near its feet was a burlap sack, tied shut. An old, chipped enamel bowl lay beside it.

But Jean-Baptiste’s eyes were drawn to the brute’s hands. They were huge, lying limp on the metal. And on the fingers of the right hand, dark against the pale skin, was a stain. Graphite. From the warehouse floor. From writing in the dirt.

NOT FIVE.

The words were gone from the dirt, but here was their residue, a smudge on the skin of the creature that had written them. It was a message, after all. A faint, accidental one. I was here. I spoke.

“Satisfied?” Gérard’s voice was at his ear.

Jean-Baptiste stepped back. “Yes. The inventory matches.” He made a show of writing on his sheet. “I’ll need your signature here, confirming the items are present and as described at time of facility handover.”

Gérard scowled but took the pen, scrawled an illegible signature on the form. “Bureaucracy,” he muttered. “A sack of rags.”

“Indeed,” Jean-Baptiste said. He folded the paper. “How long will you have to wait?”

“Who knows? They run on their own clock in there.” Gérard nodded towards the main building. A door had opened, and a man in a white coat stood silhouetted, consulting a clipboard. “Looks like they’re ready. Finally.”

The man in the white coat gestured. Another guard appeared. They walked towards the van.

Jean-Baptiste knew he had to leave. His purpose here was exhausted. But he had one more question.

“You make this run often?”

Gérard gave him a sidelong look. “Often enough.”

“Long drive. You must stop for a break. Le Relais de la Liberté, up the road?”

A flicker of something in the driver’s eyes. Caution. Confirmation. “Sometimes. Why?”

“Good coffee there?”

“It’s coffee.” Gérard’s tone closed the subject. The men from the facility were approaching. The transaction was moving from the realm of paperwork to the realm of flesh.

“My business is concluded,” Jean-Baptiste said. “Thank you for your cooperation.”

He walked back to his Renault, got in, started the engine. He did not look back as he drove away. In his mirror, he saw the rear doors of the black van swing open. He saw the two guards, with the man in the white coat supervising, begin to unstrap the brute. He saw them take hold of its arms, its legs, and lift. It was a clumsy, graceless manoeuvre. The brute’s head lolled back, its mouth slack.

They carried it towards the open door, into the building where the furnaces burned day and night.

Jean-Baptiste turned onto the main road. His hands were tight on the wheel. He had seen it. He had confirmed it. The asset was inside. The coalition was imprisoned. But he had a signature on a form. And he had a name for a café, and a name for a waitress.

He drove not back towards Paris, but back to Le Relais de la Liberté. The grey morning had hardened into a grey afternoon. He had passed from one role to the next without a pause. The clerk was gone. Now came the conspirator, armed with nothing but a desperate need and the knowledge of a trucker’s rest stop.

He pulled into the gravel lot again. The same lorries were there. He sat, watching the door. He had no plan for this. No protocol. He would have to improvise. He would have to become a different kind of man: a man who listens, who trades in kindness, or in coin.

He got out of the car. The air was cold and tasted of fried fat and exhaust. He walked towards the door of the café, towards the pretty waitress named Élise who didn’t judge, and who heard things.

The hook for the next pull was set. He was inside the grain of the wood now. The next step was to find the lever. And then, to see what splinters would fly.



---

Chapter 20

CHAPTER 20

The air inside Le Relais de la Liberté was a solid thing, a broth of decades. It was the grease of a million cheap cuts seared on the same griddle, the ghost of potatoes fried in lard that had gone rancid in ’43 and never been fully exorcised. It was the tang of unwashed bodies under wool, the acid sting of rough wine, and beneath it all, the faint, sweet-metallic scent of blood from the abattoir two kilometres down the road, carried on the boots of the men who drank here. It was not an air one breathed so much as ingested, in small, cautious sips.

Jean-Baptiste took his place at the counter, the same stool he’d occupied that morning. It was a declaration of sorts, a claim to a tiny, temporary territory. The act felt absurdly formal, like presenting credentials at a court of mud and hunger.

Élise moved behind the bar with an economy that was neither hurried nor slow. She wiped a glass with a grey cloth, her eyes on the room. She was a conductor, sensing the pressure in the atmosphere, the shift in timbre. When she came to him, she did not smile. Her glance was a swift, neutral scan—taking in the split seam of his boot, the tightness around his knuckles where they had gripped the wheel, the absence of the briefcase. He was a different man than the one who had left six hours before. The clerk had been shed, like a skin. What remained was raw, and purposefully exposed.

“Coffee,” he said. His voice was gravel in his own throat.

She nodded, turned to the urn. As she poured, she spoke without looking at him. “The pork stew is edible. The bread is from yesterday, but it’s not yet stone.”

“Just the coffee.”

She set the thick white cup before him. The liquid was black, opaque, a tar that would hold a spoon upright. He wrapped his hands around it, not for warmth, but to feel the solidity of the ceramic, an anchor in the fluid dread of the room.

He had no script. Protocol existed for dead-drops, for brush-passes, for the coded language of men who believed in systems. There was no protocol for this: for sitting at the counter of a truckers’ hellhole and trying to pry a secret from a woman who had learned, as a survival tactic, to hear everything and say nothing. He would have to trade. But he had no currency except the truth, and the truth was a grenade with the pin already pulled.

He drank the coffee. It tasted of burnt chicory and resignation.

For an hour, he was a fixture. He watched the commerce of the place. A driver with a map spread on the counter, tracing a route with a grimy finger, complaining about weigh stations. Two men in overalls from the abattoir, their forearms streaked with rusty brown, arguing in low, vehement tones about union dues. A pair of gendarmes in a corner booth, eating in sullen silence, their presence casting a temporary, brittle quiet over their corner of the room. Élise navigated it all. She refilled glasses, delivered plates, offered a non-committal hum to complaints, a flicker of a smile to a regular who left a few extra centimes on the counter. She was a mirror, reflecting back exactly what was required, revealing nothing of the glass behind the silver.

Jean-Baptiste waited. He waited for the rhythm of the place to absorb him, for the novelty of his presence to wear off. He waited for a moment when the tide of noise receded, leaving a pocket of stillness.

It came when the gendarmes left, throwing a few coins on their table with a contemptuous clatter. The door sighed shut behind them, and the room seemed to expand, to breathe out. The abattoir men returned to their argument with renewed vigour. A radio behind the bar, which had been a faint static hiss, crackled to life with a tinny Piaf song.

Élise came back to his end of the counter, began wiping it down with a wet rag. She worked her way towards him, a slow, methodical erosion of grime.

He did not look up from his cup. “The men who come here from the facility,” he said, his voice low, just above the sound of the rag on formica. “They talk.”

Her wiping did not pause. “Men talk everywhere. It’s mostly air.”

“This talk is about what they burn.”

The rag stopped. For a second, only a second, her hand went still. Then she resumed, the wet circle on the counter growing, swallowing a crumb, a stain. “Furnaces burn coal. They burn rubbish. It is not interesting talk.”

“They don’t burn rubbish at a neurological research facility.” He finally looked at her. Her face was a careful blank, but her eyes were on his, and in them was a warning, bright and sharp as a blade. “They burn people.”

The words hung in the greasy air between them. They did not echo. They were absorbed instantly, as if the room itself were hungry for such secrets, swallowing them whole. The man to his right, a trucker shovelling stew into his mouth, gave no indication he had heard. The argument about union dues continued unabated. It was as if Jean-Baptiste had spoken in a language only the two of them understood.

Élise’s lips tightened. She leaned forward, pretending to scrub at a stubborn spot. “You are a fool,” she whispered, the words hot and furious. “You bring death in here with your coffee.”

“It’s already here,” he said. “I am just giving it a name. They call them ‘the coal.’ The ones they feed to the furnaces. One of them is a friend of mine.”

She straightened up, threw the rag into a bucket beneath the counter. The clang of the pail was a full stop. Her face had shut completely, a door slammed and bolted. “Your coffee is finished. You should go.”

“I can’t.” The admission was a stone dropped from a great height. “He is in there. His name is Luc. He has a wife. A daughter who thinks he is a hero buried in an unmarked grave in Germany. He is not a hero. He is fuel for a state-owned furnace. And I have a piece of paper that says it is legal.”

For a long moment, she said nothing. She looked past him, at the room, at her kingdom of grease and exhaustion. When she spoke, her voice was flat, drained of all colour. “You think you are the first to come here with a story? You think your pain is a unique currency? Every man in this room has a story that would break your heart. They drink to forget them. I work to forget them. That is the contract.”

“I am not asking for your heart,” Jean-Baptiste said. “I am asking for a lever. A name. A shift pattern. A door that is sometimes left unbolted. Something they said when they thought the pretty waitress was just part of the furniture.”

A flicker then, in her eyes. Not sympathy, but a recognition of a shared vocabulary. She understood furniture. She understood being unseen. She picked up his empty cup, held it in both hands as if reading tea leaves in the black dregs.

“The one with the bad cough,” she said, so quietly he had to read the words on her lips as much as hear them. “Thick glasses. He smells of chemicals, not blood. He comes on Thursdays, after the evening shift change. He drinks two glasses of vin ordinaire. He sits alone. He talks to himself. He is afraid of the dark.”

Jean-Baptiste felt a click in his mind, a tiny, precise alignment. A man who smelled of chemicals. A technician. Not a guard. A man afraid of the dark, who worked where the furnaces burned.

“What does he say?”

“He says…” She closed her eyes for a second, recalling. “He says, ‘The dark ones don’t scream. It’s the ones who still know the light that make the noise.’ And once, he said, ‘The new intake is on the lower west corridor. The damp corridor. It’s quieter there.’” She opened her eyes. “That is all I have. That is all I will give. Now you must go, and you must never come back.”

He nodded. It was more than he had hoped for. A location. A lower west corridor. A damp corridor. It was a thread, fine as a spider’s silk, but a thread.

He placed a banknote on the counter, far more than the coffee was worth. A payment for information, for risk, for the shattering of her fragile contract of forgetting. She looked at it as if it were a dead insect. She did not touch it.

He stood, his joints stiff from the long stillness. As he turned to go, she spoke one last time.

“He comes tomorrow.”

Jean-Baptiste did not acknowledge her. He walked out of the café, back into the twilight. The cold air was a shock, clean and brutal after the thick interior. He got into his Renault, but he did not start the engine. He sat in the gathering dark, watching the yellow rectangle of the café window.

The conspirator had his lever. A man with a cough, thick glasses, and a fear of the dark. The next step was not to pull it, but to become part of its mechanism.
  - The following evening, just past seven, Jean-Baptiste was a ghost in the gravel lot of Le Relais. He had traded his city overcoat for a heavier, shabbier workman’s jacket bought from a stall in a market near the abattoir. It smelled of sheep and old sweat. He had not shaved. He sat in a different lorry’s shadow, watching the door.

He saw the man before he entered. He emerged from the passenger side of a battered Citroën van, huddled against the cold. He was slight, shoulders rounded as if against a perpetual wind. The thick lenses of his glasses caught the light from the window, flashing two blank, white circles. He walked with a hurried, head-down gait, and as he pushed open the café door, a cough racked his frame, a dry, tearing sound.

Jean-Baptiste waited ten minutes. He needed the man to be settled, his first glass of wine begun, the ritual of his solitude underway. Then he left the car and went inside.

The atmosphere was the same, a different cast of characters performing the same play of hunger and weariness. The man with the cough was in a booth against the far wall, as far from the door as possible. He had a glass of red wine before him, and he was staring into it, his lips moving slightly.

Jean-Baptiste did not look at him. He went to the counter. Élise was there. Their eyes met for a fraction of a second; hers held nothing, a perfect void. She served him a coffee without a word. He took it and, as if searching for a quiet spot, walked to the booth next to the coughing man. He sat with his back to him, separated only by the high, scarred wooden partition.

He could hear the man breathing, a faint, wheezing whistle underlaid by the rumble of the room. He could smell him now—not just the chemical tang, a formaldehyde sharpness, but a deeper, sour note of anxiety.

Jean-Baptiste sipped his coffee. He let the minutes stretch. He heard the man take a sip of wine, sigh, then begin the low, muttered monologue Élise had described.

“...inventory must match. Always must match. Seven units. Seven. But the dark one from Cell Twelve… the readings were different. Flickering. Like a guttering candle. Dr. Lemoine will ask… he always asks…”

A silence. The clink of glass on table.

“The damp corridor… it’s worse in winter. The walls weep. You can hear the pipes. Like… like something knocking. Wanting to get in. Or out.”

Jean-Baptiste sat perfectly still. He was a recording device, a blank page absorbing ink.

The man’s voice dropped even lower, became a conspiratorial whisper to his own reflection. “The new orderly. The big one. He laughs. He shouldn’t laugh. It’s not… it’s not a place for laughter. He says the coal is just meat. But it’s not. The ones who know the light… they are not meat. They are… they are records. And we are burning the library.”

A violent cough seized him then, doubling him over. Jean-Baptiste heard the struggle for breath, the gasp. He used the noise to shift in his seat, to let his elbow brush against the partition.

When the coughing subsided, into a wretched, wet breathing, Jean-Baptiste spoke to the wall between them, his voice a low, neutral murmur, a thought spoken aloud.

“A man can get lost in his work. Forget what he’s handling.”

There was a frozen silence from the next booth. Jean-Baptiste could feel the man’s terror, a physical wave of cold.

“Who… who is there?” The voice was thin, frayed.

“No one,” Jean-Baptiste said softly. “Just another man who hears the pipes knock. Who wonders what’s on the other side of the damp wall.”

He let the words sit. He took another sip of coffee. The ritual was everything. No sudden moves. No direct questions. You offered a fragment of their own nightmare, and waited to see if they would hand you another piece.

The man’s breathing was quick, shallow. When he spoke again, it was a desperate, rushed confession, as if the words were being forced out of him by the pressure of his own fear. “The west corridor. The last door before the service stairs. It’s not always locked. Not during the shift change at nine. The big orderly… he is lazy. He goes to smoke in the stairwell. He leaves it. For five minutes. Sometimes ten.”

Jean-Baptiste closed his eyes. The lever. The precise, applied pressure. The last door before the service stairs. Shift change at nine.

“A dangerous laziness,” Jean-Baptiste murmured. “In a place full of records.”

“They are not records!” the man hissed, a sudden, surprising venom in his whisper. “They are men! And we are… we are…”

He could not finish. Jean-Baptiste heard the scrape of his glass, the rustle of cloth as he stood up. He heard coins dropped on the table, too many, a frantic jangle. He heard the hurried, stumbling footsteps retreating, the cough erupting again as the man fled into the night.

Jean-Baptiste finished his coffee. It was cold and bitter. He left payment on the table and walked out. The Citroën van was gone.

He drove not to a hotel, but to a copse of trees two kilometres from the facility’s perimeter fence. He parked the Renault out of sight and spent the night in the car, watching the glow of the furnace chimneys against the low clouds. They burned all night. A library, turning to ash.

He thought of Luc. Not as the fiery orator, the pamphleteer, but as he might be now. A flickering candle in a damp corridor. A man who still knew the light. He thought of the wife, the daughter, their quiet pride in a martyr. He thought of the brute in the van, its head lolling, a different kind of record—one of violence and broken will. He was not saving heroes. He was retrieving wreckage from a furnace. It was a class struggle waged in the dark, over the very definition of what constituted a human being, fought with the weapons of paperwork, whispered secrets, and a lazy orderly’s smoke break.

The grey dawn arrived, leaching the colour from the world. At eight-thirty, Jean-Baptiste started the engine. He had no weapon. He had no plan beyond the door. He had only a role to play, one final part: the orderly from the morning shift, perhaps, or the lost deliveryman. He would know when he saw the door.

He drove towards the facility, the monolithic buildings growing larger, uglier, more real with every turn of the wheel. The furnaces smoked steadily. The shift would change at nine.

He was a splinter, driven deep into the grain of the state’s wood. Now, he would twist. And he would see, finally, what would break.



---

Chapter 21

CHAPTER 21

The gate was not a gate but a mouth, a slack, grey-lipped opening in a chain-link fence crowned with barbed wire that had rusted into brittle orange hooks. It was meant for service vehicles—coal trucks, laundry vans, the anonymous traffic of maintenance. A sentry box stood to one side, its windows fogged with the condensation of a guard’s breath and the steam from a portable stove. Jean-Baptiste saw the shadow of a man inside, huddled, a darker grey against the grey.

He brought the Renault to a halt, the engine idling with a tired, metallic click. The role had chosen him, as roles do for those who have emptied themselves of intention. He was not the morning orderly; they travelled in a rattling bus that had passed him twenty minutes earlier, full of slumped figures in drab coats. He was the lost deliveryman. The part required a prop. On the passenger seat lay a cardboard box, tied with twine, containing four bricks he had taken from a collapsed barn wall and wrapped in yesterday’s newspaper. The weight of it felt correct. The banality of it was armour.

The guard emerged, not with military precision, but with the grudging motion of a man pulled from a pocket of warmth. His greatcoat was too large, the cuffs frayed. He was young, with the pale, soft face of a country boy, his cheeks red from the stove’s heat. He held a clipboard listlessly.

“Papers.” The word was a puff of vapour.

Jean-Baptiste handed over the forged identity card and the work order he had concocted in the night, a thing of vague official stamps and ambiguous phrasing. Ministry of Reconstruction – Technical Supply Audit (Annexe B). It was the language of the state, designed to bore, to deflect inquiry through sheer bureaucratic weight.

The guard’s eyes moved over the words. They did not read; they performed the ritual of reading. His lips moved slightly. He looked from the papers to the box on the seat, then to Jean-Baptiste’s face. The assessment was not one of suspicion, but of inconvenience. Here was a problem that required effort. The rain chose that moment to intensify, a cold needling drizzle that soaked into the guard’s collar. He flinched.

“Annexe B is the old laundry block. Behind the main incinerator building. You can’t drive there. Road’s torn up for pipes.” He thrust the papers back. “Park there.” He pointed a gloved finger towards a patch of rutted mud already occupied by a dented lorry. “Carry it.”

Jean-Baptiste nodded, the obedient functionary. “The road?”

“Through there. Follow the fence line. You’ll see the chimney.” The guard was already turning back to his box, to his stove. The transaction was complete. The state had admitted a splinter, because the state saw only more of its own dull, wooden flesh.

Jean-Baptiste parked. He lifted the box. The bricks shifted inside with a dull, solid thud. He walked through the mouth of the gate.

The world within the fence was not a different world. It was the same world, distilled. The mud was thicker, churned by countless wheels and boots into a viscous, sucking paste that smelled of wet ash and chemical lime. The buildings were not the monolithic fortresses they appeared from a distance, but sheds grown cancerous—lean-tos of corrugated iron bolted to original stone walls, tarpaulins stretched over gaps, pipes running overhead like exposed, rusty intestines. Everything wept rust-coloured streaks. The air was a cocktail: the sweet-rot stench of the coal gas plant underlying the sharper, more alarming scent of carbolic acid, and beneath it all, the pervasive, low note of burning. Not wood, not coal. Something else.

He followed the fence, as instructed. To his left, beyond a second, inner fence of taller, newer wire, lay the core of the facility. Barracks-style dormitories with rows of small, high windows, opaque with grime. A long, low building with a reinforced door—the administration block, perhaps. And then, the incinerator building. It was larger than he had imagined, a block of stained concrete with two great iron doors, currently closed, and three chimneys from which the smoke issued not in black plumes but in a continuous, greasy, white-grey stream that flattened against the low sky. The furnaces were not just for trash. They were the digestive system of this place. They took in the waste—the soiled linen, the kitchen slop, the broken furniture, the files, the bodies—and rendered it down to a neutral ash. A library, turning to ash. The thought was no longer a metaphor. It was a description of the weather.

He passed a work detail. A line of men in striped uniforms, the fabric so thin it clung to their skeletal frames in the rain. They were moving sacks from a cart to a doorway, a slow, shuffling relay. A guard in a rain cape watched from under an eave, smoking. None of the men looked up. Their movements were automatic, drained of all quality except endurance. They were not being worked. They were being worn down, like stones in a riverbed, until they were smooth, until they were small enough to be carried away. Jean-Baptiste kept his eyes ahead, his grip tight on the box. To look too long was to invite a challenge. He was a deliveryman. He had a box. He belonged.

The old laundry block, Annexe B, was a single-storey brick building slumped against the side of the incinerator complex like a drunk against a wall. One of its windows was boarded up. The door was ajar. He stepped inside.

The heat hit him first, a damp, wool-blanket heat that carried the odour of lye and mildew. The room was vast, cavernous, filled with the ghost of machinery. Great vats, dry and rusted, stood along one wall. Conveyor belts, motionless, webbed with dust. The only light came from high, filthy windows and a single bulb dangling over a makeshift office—a desk and two filing cabinets partitioned off by sheets of plywood. A man in shirtsleeves and braces sat at the desk, his head in his hands. He did not look up.

Jean-Baptiste set the box down with a deliberate thump. The sound echoed in the hollow space.

The man raised his head. He was in his fifties, with a tired, intelligent face, glasses perched on a narrow nose. His eyes were the eyes of a clerk who has spent a lifetime navigating the minor hells of institutional failure. They took in Jean-Baptiste, the box, the lack of a proper coat or insignia.

“You’re early,” the man said. His voice was flat. “Or late. No one can ever get the schedule right.”

“Technical Supply Audit,” Jean-Baptiste said, repeating the incantation. He approached the desk, leaving the box behind. “I need to verify the manifests for the last quarter. Against the furnace logs.”

The clerk, whose nameplate read G. Lefevre, let out a sigh that seemed to come from the soles of his feet. “The furnace logs are not my department. They are kept in the main administrative block. By order of Dr. Valon.”

“I have the authorization.” Jean-Baptiste placed the work order on the desk. He kept his posture relaxed, his hands visible. He was a minor annoyance, a speck of dust in the great machine. One speck could be ignored. But a speck with paperwork required a procedure.

Lefevre put on his glasses and scanned the document. His lips pursed. “This stamp is from the Ministry of Reconstruction. The furnace operation falls under the Ministry of the Interior. Health sub-directorate. There is no jurisdiction.”

“There is an inter-ministerial agreement. For resource allocation. Coal, lime, transport. The audit is comprehensive.” Jean-Baptiste had prepared this, the gentle push into the grey areas between bureaucracies, where men like Lefevre lived and died. “My understanding is the logs are duplicated. A copy should be here, for laundry and linen throughput correlation. Annex C, paragraph two.”

He had invented Annex C. But the invention had the shape of truth. It sounded like the kind of redundant, nonsensical regulation that governed this place.

Lefevre stared at him. A war played out on his tired face: the instinct to refuse, pitted against the deeper, more powerful instinct to avoid responsibility, to pass the problem along. To have a stranger sign for a document and leave was preferable to a complaint, an inquiry, a mark on his own record.

“Paragraph two,” Lefevre repeated, testing the words. He sighed again. He stood, his chair scraping on the concrete floor. “The duplicate logs. They are not collated. They are in the archive room. In the back. It is a mess. You will find what you need yourself. I have the monthly linen report to finish. I cannot be your guide.”

“I require only a signature of receipt,” Jean-Baptiste said, following him around the plywood partition towards a heavy metal door. “Once I have verified the figures.”

“Verify what you like. The numbers never match. Nothing here matches.” Lefevre produced a large ring of keys, selected one, and unlocked the door. He pushed it open. The air that drifted out was cold and carried the smell of old paper and concrete dust. “The light switch is inside, to the left. Do not move anything from its pile. The order, such as it is, is known only to me.”

Jean-Baptiste stepped into the archive room. Lefevre did not follow. The door closed behind him. He heard the key turn in the lock.

It was not a room for archives. It was a tomb for paper. Boxes were stacked haphazardly, spilling files. Ledgers were piled on makeshift shelves of planks and bricks. In one corner, a mountain of loose forms and carbon copies rose nearly to the ceiling, a paper avalanche frozen in mid-fall. The single bulb, when he found the switch, cast a weak, jaundiced light.

He had not come for furnace logs.

He moved to the back of the chaos, to the oldest-looking stacks. These were not the standardized forms of the current administration, but older, ledger books with leather spines, logbooks from when this place was a sanatorium, before the war, before the new wire. He ran a finger along the spines, his heart a slow, heavy drum in his chest. Patient Admissions, 1938-1939. Kitchen Inventory, 1940. Medical Supplies.

And then: Transfer & Discharge Registry, 1941-Present.

He pulled the heavy volume from its shelf. The dust made a cloud in the lamplight. He opened it on a precarious stack of boxes.

The pages were ruled columns, filled in a variety of hands—the neat script of a head nurse, the hurried scrawl of a night orderly, the bureaucratic print of an administrator. Names. Dates of birth. Dates of admission. Transfer From. Transfer To. Remarks.

His eyes scanned the entries from the last six months. The ‘Transfer To’ column was rarely filled. More often, the ‘Remarks’ column held a single, stark notation. Deceased. Deceased. Deceased. Cause of death was seldom listed. Sometimes: Cardiac failure. Pulmonary complication. General debility.

He turned the pages back, his fingers leaving smudges in the grime. Back through the spring, the winter. Back to October. The names blurred into a river of the condemned. He was looking for a pattern not of life, but of disappearance. A transfer that was not a death.

And then he saw it.

Lemaire, Luc. DOB: 12/04/1905. Adm: 22/09/1950. Transfer From: Fresnes Prison. Remarks: Section VII. Special Observation.

Section VII. The words were written in a different ink, a tighter, more authoritative hand. There was no date of death. No transfer out.

He carefully closed the ledger. He had a name. He had a section. It was more than he had come with. It was a thread, pulled from the fabric of the place. He needed to find where that thread led.

He went to the door and knocked, three firm raps.

After a moment, the key turned. Lefevre stood there, his expression one of profound resignation. “You are finished?”

“The correlation is incomplete,” Jean-Baptiste said, stepping out. “The logs for October are missing. The critical month. I will need to consult the primary set in the administrative block.”

Lefevre’s face tightened. “That is impossible. I cannot authorize access to the main block. You must take your incomplete audit and file a variance report.”

“A variance of this magnitude triggers a full procedural review,” Jean-Baptiste said, the threat implicit, cloaked in jargon. “The audit team would be here for a week. Interviewing all departmental heads. Including you.”

The colour drained from Lefevre’s face. The spectre of scrutiny, of questions he could not answer, of systems he knew were rotten being poked at by outsiders, was his personal nightmare. “Dr. Valon does not see unscheduled personnel.”

“Then schedule it. A telephone call. Tell him it is a five-minute verification. From the Ministry of Reconstruction. He will understand the implications of a delay.”

Lefevre wavered. He looked at his desk, at his unfinished report, at the rain streaking the windows. He saw a week of hell against five minutes of another man’s annoyance. He went to the telephone on his desk, a heavy, black apparatus. He cranked the handle, asked for an extension. His voice was low, obsequious.

“Yes, sir. A moment of your time, sir. A minor audit point… Yes, sir. Reconstruction… I understand, sir. He is here now. Thank you, sir.”

He hung up. He looked at Jean-Baptiste as if he were a disease he had been forced to catch. “He will see you. For five minutes. The main block, second floor. Room 217. Do not be late. Do not wander. Go directly there and directly back.”

Jean-Baptiste nodded. He left the box of bricks on the floor of the laundry. He walked back out into the rain, towards the inner fence.

This gate was different. A uniformed guard, older, with a pistol on his belt, stood in a proper booth. He checked a clipboard, made a telephone call of his own. He eyed Jean-Baptiste with a colder, more professional detachment than the boy at the outer gate. This was the inner sanctum, where the fiction of the state was maintained with sharper tools.

“Straight to the main entrance. Up the stairs. Second floor. Do not deviate,” the guard said, unlocking a pedestrian gate.

The administrative block had once aspired to something. A municipal building, perhaps, or a school. Its stone steps were worn in the middle. The foyer was a vault of echoing space, lit by a single, large window overlooking the yard. A portrait of the President hung crookedly on one wall. The air was still, cold, smelling of floor polish and stale tobacco. A clerk at a reception desk pointed wordlessly to the staircase.

The stairs were marble, chipped at the edges. His footsteps echoed. On the second floor, a long corridor stretched in both directions, doors at regular intervals, each with a number and sometimes a nameplate. Records. Accounting. Personnel. The air was warmer here, heated by radiators that clanked and hissed. It was the sound of managed comfort, a world away from the damp heat of the laundry or the sucking mud of the yard.

Room 217. The nameplate read: Dr. A. Valon, Deputy Director (Medical Services).

Jean-Baptiste knocked.

“Enter.”

The voice was crisp, educated, devoid of regional accent. A Parisian voice, from the right arrondissement.

The office was a study in controlled authority. Neat bookshelves held medical texts and bound reports. A large, tidy desk. A window looked out over the inner compound, a strategic view of the kingdom. The man behind the desk was in his forties, with fine, silvering hair brushed back from a high forehead. He wore a well-cut suit, not a uniform. He did not look up from the file he was reading, holding a silver pen poised above it.

“You have five minutes,” Dr. Valon said. “Explain this variance.”

Jean-Baptiste took two steps into the room. He did not approach the desk. He stood, allowing his damp coat to drip on the polished floor. He was the intrusion, the grit from the outside world. “The October furnace logs. The duplicate set in Annexe B is missing. I require the primary set to complete the coal consumption audit. The figures are critical for the Reconstruction Ministry’s resource allocation model.”

Valon finally looked up. His eyes were a pale, watery blue. They moved over Jean-Baptiste, not with the guard’s assessment of threat, nor Lefevre’s look of harassed defeat. This was a clinical gaze. A categorizing gaze. It measured, it placed, it filed.

“The allocation model,” Valon repeated. He set his pen down carefully. “A fascinating priority, while men starve in the streets of Lyon. But we must all serve the machinery, mustn’t we?” He smiled, a thin, professional curve of the lips that did not touch his eyes. “The logs are in the records office, down the hall. I will have them brought to you. Wait outside.”

It was a dismissal. Clean, efficient. No argument, no engagement. The splinter was to be extracted by a subordinate and discarded.

Jean-Baptiste did not move. “The ledger I saw referenced a Section VII. For special observation. Does that section have its own supply line? Its own furnace metrics? The audit must be compartmentally complete.”

The air in the room changed. It did not grow colder; it grew still. The faint hiss of the radiator seemed to amplify. Valon’s smile did not vanish, but it solidified, like a crack in ice.

“Section VII is a clinical classification. Not a logistical one. It falls outside the purview of a coal audit.” Valon’s voice remained even, but a new quality had entered it—a sharp, surgical precision. “Who exactly in Reconstruction issued this mandate?”

“The mandate is inter-ministerial. Signed at the deputy level.” Jean-Baptiste held the man’s gaze. He was in the grey area now, the place where paperwork ended and will began.

“A name,” Valon said softly.

“The audit code is sufficient. R-50 slash B.”

“R-50 slash B.” Valon leaned back in his chair, steepling his fingers. The clinical gaze was now a dissection. “An interesting code. Used primarily for infrastructure assessment of decommissioned facilities. Not for active operational supply.” He paused. “Your identity card, please. The one you showed at the gate. I wish to note the inspector number for my files.”

It was a trap, sprung with elegant simplicity. The forgery would not hold under this specific, informed scrutiny. The fiction was unravelling.

Jean-Baptiste felt the moment crystallize. The door behind him was closed. The corridor outside was quiet. He had no weapon. He had only the role, and the role was now a torn costume.

“The card is with the gate guard,” he said, taking a half-step backward, towards the door.

“It will have been recorded. I will telephone down.” Valon’s hand moved towards the telephone on his desk.

There was no decision. There was only the fracture of the plan. Jean-Baptiste turned, his hand reaching for the door handle.

“Guard!” Valon’s voice, raised for the first time, was not a shout. It was a command, clear and carrying.

Jean-Baptiste yanked the door open. The corridor was still empty. But from the staircase well, he heard the quick, heavy tread of boots ascending. More than one pair.

He went left, away from the stairs, deeper into the corridor. Doors flashed by. 218. 219. He tried a handle. Locked. The boot-steps reached the top of the stairs, turned into the corridor behind him. A voice called out, “Halt!”

He ran. The corridor ended in a T-junction. Right led towards a window overlooking a rear courtyard. Left led to another, narrower corridor and a sign: Service Stair – Authorized Only.

He took the service stair. It was concrete, uncarpeted, lit by a wire-caged bulb. He took the steps two at a time, descending. Not towards the ground floor, but further down. The air grew colder, damper. The smell of carbolic acid grew stronger, mixed with a faint, sweet-sour odour he could not place.

The stairwell ended in a basement corridor. Pipes ran along the low ceiling. The floor was bare concrete, slick with condensation. One direction led to a heavy metal door marked Boiler Room. The other ended at a pair of swinging doors with small, reinforced glass windows.

He could hear voices from above, on the stairs. He moved towards the swinging doors.

Through the glass, he saw a long, tiled room. A hospital ward, but stripped of any comfort. Iron bedframes, bolted to the floor, were spaced in two rows. Most were empty. A few held figures, lying still under thin grey blankets. A nurse in a stiff, white cap sat at a desk near the far door, writing in a ledger. The light was flat, fluorescent, humming faintly.

This was not Section VII. This was the general infirmary. The antechamber.

The door at the top of the service stair banged open. “Check down there!”

Jean-Baptiste pushed through the swinging doors.

The smell hit him fully now: antiseptic, illness, stale sweat, and that underlying sweet-sour note—the smell of infected flesh, of starvation. The nurse looked up, her face a mask of routine surprise that hardened into suspicion. She was a large woman, with powerful forearms.

“You are not authorized,” she stated, beginning to rise.

“Dr. Valon sent me,” Jean-Baptiste said, walking forward, his voice assuming a brittle authority. “An incident with an auditor. He may be attempting to access the secure sections. You are to lock this ward down. No one in or out until the all-clear.”

He invoked the hierarchy. He used the language of emergency and procedure. Her suspicion warred with training. Dr. Valon’s name was a key.

“Secure sections?” she repeated, her hand pausing on the telephone.

“Section VII. He may try to pose as staff. You have seen no one?”

She shook her head, her eyes wide. “No one. The day shift is not due for another hour.”

“Lock the door behind me. I will check the auxiliary supply corridor.” He pointed to the door at the far end of the ward.

She nodded, moving to the main doors he had entered, fumbling with a key from her belt.

Jean-Baptiste walked swiftly between the rows of beds. He kept his eyes forward, but his peripheral vision took in the occupants. Grey faces, sunken eyes, mouths open in sleep or stupor. One man watched him pass, his gaze tracking him with a dull, animal awareness. There was no recognition, only a faint curiosity, like a fish observing a shadow pass over its tank.

He reached the far door. It was not locked. He pushed through.

He was in another corridor, lower, darker. The pipes here were thicker, hotter to the touch. The sound of machinery was a constant thrum. This was the circulatory system of the facility. And at the end of this corridor was another door, heavy, metal, with a wheel lock like a pressure hatch. Above it, stencilled in faded paint, was the designation: SECTION VII – OBSERVATION. AUTHORIZED PERSONNEL ONLY.

This was it. The thread’s end.

He heard the ward door open behind him, the nurse’s voice calling out, “Sir? The doors are locked, but—”

He did not wait. He grabbed the wheel lock. It was cold, stiff. He threw his weight into it. It groaned, resisted, then gave with a shriek of unoiled metal. He pulled the door open.

The space beyond was not a corridor. It was a large, circular chamber, a former utility vault perhaps. The walls were curved concrete. In the centre of the room stood a single, high-backed chair, like a dentist’s chair, but heavier, with leather straps on the arms and legs. It was bolted to the floor. A tray of instruments—not surgical, but electrical, with dials and wires—stood on a trolley beside it. A single, bright lamp on a stand was directed at the chair, leaving the rest of the room in deep shadow.

And in the chair sat a man.

He was thin, so thin the shape of his skull was visible beneath the tight-stretched skin of his face. His hair was patchy, grey. He wore a loose, grey smock. His head was slumped forward on his chest. But as the door opened, as the light from the corridor spilled in, the head lifted.

The eyes found Jean-Baptiste. They were not dull. They were not animal. They were lucid, burning with a fierce, exhausted intelligence. They were the eyes of a man who had been burned down to his core, and in that core, a single, unquenchable flame still flickered.

It was Luc Lemaire.

For a second, there was no recognition. Then, a slow, painful focusing. The cracked lips parted.

“Jean-Baptiste?” The voice was a dry whisper, the sound of leaves scraping on stone. It was not a question of hope. It was a statement of surreal, impossible fact.

Behind him, Jean-Baptiste heard shouts, the sound of the ward door being unlocked, many boots on tile.

He stepped into the room, letting the heavy door swing shut behind him. The lock engaged with a solid, final clunk. They were sealed in, for a moment, in the vault.

He crossed the space to the chair. He saw the raw marks on Luc’s wrists where the straps had chafed. He saw the bruise-like shadows under his eyes. He saw the man who had written the pamphlets that had lit fires in the minds of dockworkers and seamstresses, reduced to a specimen in a concrete cell.

“We have to go. Now.” Jean-Baptiste’s hands went to the straps, fumbling with the buckles. They were stiff, secured with small padlocks.

Luc’s hand, light as a bird’s claw, came up and closed around Jean-Baptiste’s wrist. The grip was weak, but it stopped him. “No.” The whisper was urgent. “You cannot… take me from this chair.”

“They are coming.”

“Listen.” Luc’s eyes held his, the flame in them guttering but intense. “They do not break you with pain. Not here. They break you with… the offer. The bargain.” He took a shuddering breath. “They show you a man. A comrade. In the next room. They give you a switch. You can give the man a shock. A small one. Or you can take it for him. You choose. Every hour, you choose.”

Jean-Baptiste stared, the horror of it dawning—not a torture of the body, but of the conscience. A class struggle waged on the soul.

“I took it,” Luc whispered, a terrible pride in his ruined voice. “For three days, I took it. Then… they brought in a child. A boy from the dormitory. They said he was a thief. They gave me the switch.” His eyes glistened in the lamp’s glare. “Jean-Baptiste… I gave the shock. To save myself for the cause. I gave it. And they laughed. There was no current. The boy was an actor. They wanted to see the hero make the choice of a coward. They have it on a wire recording. They play it for me. To show me what I am.”

The door shuddered as something heavy struck it from the outside.

“They have won,” Luc said, the words final as a stone dropping into a well. “The wreckage is not just the body. It is the idea of yourself. Leave me. Tell them… tell them the martyr is dead. He died in this chair.”

Jean-Baptiste’s fingers found the padlock on the last strap. He had no tool. He looked around, his gaze sweeping the shadowed room. On the instrument trolley, among the dials and wires, was a heavy, insulated pair of pliers.

The door shook again, a thunderous blow. A voice, Valon’s, filtered through the metal. “Open the door. There is no other exit. Open it, and his treatment ends today. He can have a bed. He can have soup.”

A bargain.

Jean-Baptiste grabbed the pliers. He fitted them to the padlock. He squeezed with all his strength. The metal groaned.

Luc watched him, his expression one of infinite sadness. “You are retrieving wreckage.”

The hasp of the padlock snapped with a sharp ping.

Jean-Baptiste tore the strap free. He put an arm under Luc’s shoulders, hauling him up from the chair. The man was a bundle of sticks, impossibly light. “The wreckage is all we have,” he grunted. “The furnace gets the rest.”

He half-carried, half-dragged Luc towards the curved wall, away from the door. There was no other exit. The door was the only way in or out. They were in a stone bottle.

The blows on the door became rhythmic, a battering ram. The metal began to buckle inwards.

Jean-Baptiste’s back found the cold concrete wall. He held Luc upright, facing the door. The light from the single lamp cast their elongated shadows against the wall, two thin, merging shapes.

The final blow tore the lock from its housing. The door screamed open, framing three guards in the doorway, truncheons in hand. Behind them, in the brighter light of the corridor, stood Dr. Valon, his face a calm, expectant mask.

The guards surged in.

Jean-Baptiste did not move. He stood, supporting Luc, a human shield made of bone and will. He had no weapon. He had no plan. He had only the fact of his presence, a splinter that had twisted, and reached the heart of the wood.

The first guard raised his truncheon.

And from the darkness of the corridor behind Valon, a new shape lunged. It was big, fast, a blur of motion. A meaty fist connected with the side of the lead guard’s head with a wet crack. The guard crumpled.

The brute from the Citroën van stood in the doorway, his face a mask of brutal efficiency. He held a heavy wrench. His eyes met Jean-Baptiste’s for a fraction of a second—no recognition, no camaraderie, only the cold focus of a tradesman on a job.

He had not come for Jean-Baptiste. He had come for the package.

Chaos erupted. The two remaining guards turned on the new threat. The brute moved with shocking speed for his size, the wrench a blur, breaking an arm, crushing a knee. Valon was shouting, backing away, his clinical composure shattered.

The brute stepped over the fallen guards. He looked at Luc, then at Jean-Baptiste. “Can he walk?”

“No.”

The brute nodded. In one motion, he slung Luc over his shoulder like a sack of grain. He turned, wrench still in hand, and kicked the fallen door out of his way. “Follow. Or don’t.”

He plunged back into the corridor, moving towards the service stair.

Jean-Baptiste followed, his mind a blank of adrenaline. They passed the infirmary ward, where the nurse cowered behind her desk. They took the stairs up, not down, back towards the ground floor. At the top, the brute kicked open the door to the main corridor.

It was empty. The alarm had not yet spread here.

They moved towards the front entrance, a grotesque procession: the brute with his living burden, Jean-Baptiste trailing, his ears ringing with the aftermath of violence.

They burst into the foyer. The clerk at the desk stood up, his mouth open.

“Fire in the boiler room!” the brute roared, his voice filling the space. “Get out! Evacuate!”

The clerk hesitated, then the instinct for self-preservation took over. He ran for the main doors.

They followed, out into the rain, down the worn steps, across the yard towards the inner gate. The guard there was already confused, seeing the running clerk, seeing the giant with a body on his shoulder.

“Medical emergency!” the brute bellowed, not breaking stride. “Contagion! Open the gate!”

The guard, young, faced with the sheer momentum of the lie, fumbled with the lock. The gate swung open.

They were through. They crossed the muddy no-man’s-land towards the outer service gate. The boy guard was out of his box, staring at the running figures, at the chaos unfolding in the inner compound.

The brute did not slow. He hit the chain-link gate at a run, his shoulder smashing it open, the rusted wire shrieking in protest.

The Renault was where Jean-Baptiste had left it. The brute yanked open the back door and deposited Luc onto the seat. He turned to Jean-Baptiste, thrusting the car keys into his hand. They were slick with rain, or blood.

“The van is on the road north, two kilometres. A blue Peugeot truck will meet you at the crossroads in one hour. They have the papers, the route. Go.”

“Who are you?” Jean-Baptiste asked, the rain streaming down his face.

The brute’s expression did not change. “A man who got tired of moving furniture.” He looked back towards the facility. Sirens were beginning to wail, a thin, desperate sound against the rain. “They will lock the district down in twenty minutes. You have nineteen. Go.”

He turned and loped away, disappearing into the grey curtain of rain towards the perimeter fence.

Jean-Baptiste got into the Renault. He looked into the back seat. Luc was curled on his side, his eyes closed, his breathing shallow. Alive. Wreckage.

He started the engine. He drove away from the gate, the mud spraying from the tires. In the rearview mirror, he saw the grey buildings recede, the chimneys still smoking, digesting the world. He had twisted the splinter. And the wood had not broken. It had yielded, for a moment, a crack just wide enough for a broken man to slip through.

But the crack would close. The machinery would right itself, file a report, adjust a procedure. It would continue to burn. And it would remember the splinter.

He drove north, towards the crossroads, towards the blue Peugeot truck, towards the next link in the chain. The rain fell, the same grey, persistent judgement. It found the cracks. It always found the cracks.



---

Chapter 22

CHAPTER 22

The blue Peugeot truck was the colour of a faded bruise. It waited at the crossroads like a patient beast, its engine a low, bronchial rumble in the rain. The crossroads was not a place, but an absence: a flat stretch of mud between skeletal poplars, a junction of two farm tracks that led to other tracks, a geometry of obscurity. The Renault’s headlights swept over it, and the Peugeot flashed its own lights once, a signal as brief and functional as a bolt sliding home.

Jean-Baptiste pulled the Renault alongside. The driver’s door of the Peugeot opened and a woman stepped down. She wore a man’s oilskin coat, belted tight, and a scarf knotted under her chin. Her face was all angles and shadows, carved by weather and something harder. She didn’t offer a name. She looked past Jean-Baptiste to the back seat of the Renault, her gaze clinical.

“Is he conscious?”

“No.”

“Good. Less trouble.” She jerked her thumb towards the back of the Peugeot. “Transfer him. Quickly.”

The rear of the truck was a shell, empty but for two straw pallets and a steel drum bolted to the floor. It smelled of diesel and wet hay. Together, they lifted Luc. He was a dead weight, a sack of bones and feverish heat. They laid him on one pallet. The woman threw a rough wool blanket over him, not for comfort, but for concealment. She moved with an efficiency that spoke of practice, of a life spent moving contraband—whether it was people, pamphlets, or pork cuts across a ration line, it was all the same mechanics of evasion.

She slammed the rear doors and turned to Jean-Baptiste. “The Renault stays here. Keys on the seat. It will be collected, erased. You ride up front with me. You are my brother, we are delivering machine parts to a cooperative in Amiens. The papers are in the glovebox. Memorise the names, the dates. If we are stopped, you are tired, you are simple, you let me talk.”

“And if they look in the back?”

“Then the papers will not matter.” She lit a cigarette, the match flaring in the damp gloom. “Your friend in the rain said you had a talent for silence. Use it.”

She climbed back into the driver’s seat. Jean-Baptiste stood for a moment in the mud. The Renault, its engine ticking as it cooled, was the last object linking him to the life he had methodically dismantled. He placed the keys on the worn leather of the driver’s seat. He did not look back. He got into the passenger side of the Peugeot.

The cab was close, warm with the smell of tobacco, damp wool, and the faint, sharp scent of fear that no amount of washing could ever quite erase from the upholstery of these vehicles. The woman engaged the gear with a solid crunch, and they lurched onto the northern track.

“The route is circuitous,” she said, not taking her eyes from the ruts ahead. “We avoid the national roads. Patrols. Checkpoints. We travel the roads that serve the farms, the factories. The roads of work. They are watched less closely. The authorities assume nothing of consequence moves on them.”

Jean-Baptiste watched the skeletal landscape pass. Fields, fallow and black, drank the rain. Isolated farmsteads huddled under slate roofs, their windows dark. This was the other France, the one that existed in the margins of the maps the Préfecture used. A France of subsistence and suspicion, where a strange truck was noted, its passage filed away in the silent ledgers of peasant memory. It was a landscape of class without rhetoric, a simple, grinding fact of distance from the centre.

They drove for an hour in silence. The rhythm of the wipers, the growl of the engine, the hiss of tires on wet gravel—it was a lullaby of transit. Jean-Baptiste felt the artificial clarity of the escape begin to dissolve, replaced by a profound, cellular fatigue. He had not slept in two days. His hands, resting on his knees, trembled slightly. He willed them still.

“He will need a doctor,” Jean-Baptiste said, his voice rough from disuse.

“He will have one. At the next link. Not a hospital doctor. A union doctor. A man who understands that wounds are not always accidental, and that files are not always opened.” She flicked ash out the narrow vent window. “Your friend. What did they want from him?”

“To burn what was left of his mind. To make him a tool.”

She nodded, as if this was a commonplace order. “And you? What are you?”

It was the question the brute had not asked. Jean-Baptiste looked at his reflection in the rain-beaded window—a pale smudge, a ghost in the glass. “I was a functionary. A clerk of the furnace.”

“And now you are a splinter.”

He turned to look at her. Her profile was sharp against the grey light.

“The man in the rain. He said you were the splinter that got twisted. He said the machinery would remember.” She took a long drag. “It always does. It has a long memory, and very few teeth of its own. It rents them from men in uniforms. Those men have short memories, but excellent filing systems.”

The truck bounced through a deep pothole, jolting them. From the back, a low moan filtered through the partition.

“He’s waking,” she said. “It’s better if he doesn’t. Not yet. Not until we are off this road.” She nodded to a canvas bag at Jean-Baptiste’s feet. “Syringe and a vial. Morphine. From the war. Do it.”

Jean-Baptiste bent and rummaged in the bag. He found the small metal case. Inside, on faded velvet, lay a glass syringe and two ampoules. Tools of a different kind of mercy. He filled the syringe, the liquid clear and indifferent.

“You do it,” he said.

“I am driving. You have the hands for it. Precise hands. Clerk’s hands.”

He moved into the back, crouching in the swaying space. Luc’s eyes were half-open, but they saw nothing, rolling white in the gloom. His skin was hot and damp. Jean-Baptiste pushed up the sleeve of the filthy tunic. The arm was whipcord thin, the veins prominent. He was not unfamiliar with the procedure. During the war, he had seen it done in field stations, the quick prick, the blessed emptiness that followed. A administrative solution to the problem of pain.

He inserted the needle. Depressed the plunger. Luc’s body gave a slight shudder, then settled into a deeper, more profound stillness. The breathing eased. Jean-Baptiste remained crouched, watching the man he had pulled from the fire. What had he saved? A body. A history. A set of memories that were now weapons, both for and against him. He was a document, retrieved from the incinerator at the last possible second, edges charred, text smoke-damaged and fragile.

He returned to the front. The woman glanced at him. “You did not hesitate.”

“It was a prescribed action. I am good at those.”

She almost smiled. “A clerk to the last.”

They drove on. The rain lessened to a mist, a fine gauze over the world. They passed through villages where life seemed to have been suctioned out, leaving only the shells of houses and a few old women in black, watching from doorways. They crossed a canal on an iron bridge that shuddered under their weight. The water below was the colour of lead.

As evening began to stain the sky a deeper grey, she turned onto a slightly wider road, lined with leafless, pollarded trees. “This is the Zone,” she said quietly. “The edge of the coal country. The soil is poor, the air is sour. The people work underground, or in the chemical plants. They cough. They die young. They unionise. The authorities watch them like a pathologist watches a diseased lung.”

The houses changed. They were row upon row of identical brick cottages, stained with soot, their small windows glowing with a weak, yellow light. The air coming through the vent now carried a new smell: the acrid tang of coal smoke mixed with the rotten-egg stench of industrial sulphur.

“We stop at the house with the blue door. The third in the row after the estaminet. You will carry him inside. I will unload the legitimate cargo—the machine parts. It provides the cover. You will have fifteen minutes with the doctor. Then you move again. The next truck arrives at twenty hundred hours. A meat delivery van. You will be part of that delivery.”

“How many links in this chain?” Jean-Baptiste asked.

“As many as it takes to get you to a port, or a border, or a hole deep enough to disappear into. The chain is only as strong as its weakest compartment. We do not know the other compartments. It is safer that way.”

She pulled the truck over with a smooth, unhurried motion, stopping outside a soot-blackened cottage. A faint blue paint was just visible on the lower half of the door. The estaminet on the corner was noisy, spilling light and the sound of accordion music into the street. The sound was aggressively cheerful, a forced gaiety over a deep well of silence.

As Jean-Baptiste moved to the back, the woman caught his arm. Her grip was strong. “In there,” she said, nodding to the blue door, “you will see the cost. Do not look away. It is the price of the ticket you are riding.”

He lifted Luc out, staggering under the weight. The blue door opened before he reached it, and a man in a sleeveless woollen vest, his arms thick and knotted with muscle, took Luc from him with ease. Inside, it was dark, hot, and smelled of cabbage soup, carbolic soap, and illness.

The main room was a kitchen. A stout woman with her hair in a tight bun stirred a pot on a cast-iron range. She did not look up. The man carried Luc through to a back room, laid him on a narrow bed. Another man was there, older, with a tired, intelligent face and sleeves rolled up. The doctor. He set to work immediately, checking Luc’s pulse, peeling back his eyelids, examining the wounds on his wrists and the newer, uglier marks on his temples.

Jean-Baptiste stood in the doorway, an obstruction. The doctor spoke without looking at him. “Dehydration. Exhaustion. Infection in the abrasions. Signs of systemic shock. And the cranial marks… electrodes. They were mapping him. Or unmaking him.” He prepared an injection of penicillin. “He will live. Whether he will be the man you knew is a question for philosophers, not doctors.”

The man who had carried Luc in lit a pipe. He looked at Jean-Baptiste. “You came from the Saint-Cloud facility.”

It was not a question. Jean-Baptiste nodded.

“A fortress. How?”

“A man inside. A labourer. He opened a gate.”

The man grunted, a sound of deep satisfaction. “The brute. Good. They think the walls are to keep us out. They are also to keep them in. Sometimes, the furniture moves itself.” He gestured with his pipe stem towards the front room. “My son. He worked at the nitrate plant. A vat ruptured. The chemicals… they ate his lungs. The company doctor called it a pre-existing condition. The insurance assessor, a clerk from Paris, agreed. The file was closed.”

Jean-Baptiste followed his gaze. In the front room, in a chair by the stove, a young man sat wrapped in a blanket. He was maybe twenty. His face was waxy, his eyes distant. Each breath was a deliberate, rasping effort, a slow bellows pumping air through sodden tissue. The sound filled the small house, a metronome of loss.

“He is not a casualty of war,” the man said, his voice low and flat. “He is a by-product of peace. A line on a profit ledger. Your friend in there,” he nodded towards Luc, “he was a by-product of a different process. But it is the same ledger. They just use different columns.”

The doctor finished his ministrations. “He will sleep for eight hours. The fever should break. He cannot be moved until then.”

“We must be on the meat truck at twenty hundred,” Jean-Baptiste said.

“Then you will go. He will stay. He will follow when he can. A different link.” The doctor wiped his hands on a towel. “Compartmentalisation. You are now a separate item. Your passage is arranged.”

Jean-Baptiste looked at Luc, asleep on the narrow bed. He had pulled him this far, through wire and rain, only to be severed from him at a soot-stained kitchen table. It was the logic of the chain. It was the only logic that worked.

The woman from the truck appeared in the back doorway. “Time.”

The man with the pipe stood. “Come. You will eat something before you go. It is a long night.”

They sat at the kitchen table. The stout woman placed two bowls of cabbage soup and a slab of coarse bread before them. She still did not speak. The rasping breath of the young man in the chair was the only conversation. Jean-Baptiste ate. The soup was thin, salty, sustaining. It was the food of endurance.

The man spoke between puffs on his pipe. “The meat truck will take you to the abattoir on the outskirts of Lille. You will be met. From there, perhaps the docks. Perhaps the Belgian border. They do not tell me. But remember this house. Remember the cost. It is not abstract. It is this.” He did not gesture to his son. He did not need to. The sound said it all.

At five minutes to eight, the woman stood. Jean-Baptiste followed her to the door. He looked back once at the room where Luc slept. The doctor was sitting in a chair beside the bed, reading a newspaper by the light of a single bulb. A scene of bizarre domestic normality.

Outside, the night was cold and thick with industrial fog. The accordion music from the estaminet had grown louder, more desperate. A meat truck, painted a grimy white, idled further down the street. Its sides were streaked with dark, viscous stains.

The woman shook his hand. Her grip was firm, final. “Good luck, clerk. Try to become more than a splinter. Try to become a spark.”

She walked back to her Peugeot, a silhouette swallowed by the fog. Jean-Baptiste approached the meat truck. The rear doors were open. The interior was a cave of darkness, smelling of blood, salt, and cold iron. Shapes of carcasses hung from hooks, swaying slightly with the idle of the engine.

A face appeared in the gloom—young, nervous, with a butcher’s apron tied over his coat. “In here. Quickly. Lie down between the rows. Do not move. Do not make a sound. The journey is two hours. There are two checkpoints. We are a regular run. They know the truck.”

Jean-Baptiste climbed in. The floor was slick with a thin, frozen film. He lay down in the narrow space between two rows of hanging sides of beef. The cold seeped through his clothes instantly. The butcher leaned in.

“If they open the doors, you are a stain on the floor. You are nothing. Understood?”

Jean-Baptiste nodded. The butcher swung the doors shut. The latch clanged with a terrible finality. Darkness was absolute. The sound of the engine grew muffled, distant. The only immediate sensations were the penetrating cold of the steel floor, the dense, metallic smell of death, and the gentle, pendulous sway of the slaughtered animals above him.

The truck moved off. He was a parcel again, being routed through the veins of the industrial north. A piece of contraband human material. He thought of the brute, walking back into the rain towards the fence. A man who got tired of moving furniture. He thought of the woman in the oilskin, driving her circuits of obscurity. He thought of the man with the pipe, and his son’s ruined breath. A by-product. A line in a ledger.

The machinery had yielded a crack. He had slipped through. But the crack did not lead to freedom. It led to this: a deeper labyrinth within the belly of the machine itself, a labyrinth built from compartmentalised pity and cold professionalism. He was not escaping to anything. He was being processed through. The destination was irrelevant. The transit was the point. To keep moving, to be a problem in logistics for the other side, to force a series of small, illegal adjustments in the great, grinding schedule of the state.

He shivered in the dark. Above him, the beef swayed, their frozen flanks sometimes brushing his shoulder with a touch like stone. He counted the turns of the truck. He counted the times it slowed. He waited for the shout, the bang on the doors, the blade of torchlight.

At the first checkpoint, the truck stopped. Voices. Muffled laughter. The familiar ritual of papers. The butcher’s voice, young and eager to please. The clink of a bottle being offered. A joke about the quality of the meat. The voices faded. The truck moved on.

The relief was not a warmth, but a deeper cold. It was the cold of understanding. He was not a hero. He was not even a rebel. He was an administrative error, a fugitive file, being smuggled from one forgotten drawer to another by a network of people who had learned that the only way to fight a system that viewed them as inventory was to master the art of mis-filing.

The second checkpoint was quieter. More formal. Questions about the manifest. A longer pause. Jean-Baptiste held his breath. His muscles ached with the strain of stillness. Above him, a hook creaked. A single drop of thawing ice or blood landed on his forehead. He did not wipe it away.

The doors remained closed. The truck lurched forward again.

He lost track of time in the dark, in the cold, in the rhythmic sway. The fatigue was a weight now, pulling him down into the steel. He fought it. To sleep was to lose control, to risk a sound. He fixed his mind on the sound from the blue-doored house—the rasping, deliberate breath. A by-product. A cost. It was a hook in his mind, keeping him present in the freezing dark.

The truck’s motion changed. It slowed, turned onto a smoother surface, then onto cobbles. It made several sharp turns. It stopped. The engine cut.

Silence.

Then, the sound of the driver’s door opening and closing. Footsteps on cobbles. A key in the lock.

The rear doors swung open. The butcher’s face was there, pale in the weak light of a single yard lamp. Behind him, Jean-Baptiste could see the high, dark walls of a courtyard, the glint of wet cobblestones.

“Out. Now.”

Jean-Baptiste crawled out, his limbs stiff and protesting. He stood in the courtyard of the abattoir. The air was a solid wall of stench—blood, offal, lye, and the sweet, nauseating smell of rendered fat. Great brick buildings loomed, their windows dark. Pipes dripped. Somewhere, steam hissed.

The butcher pointed to a low doorway, lit by a bare bulb. “In there. Wait.”

Jean-Baptiste walked to the doorway. It led into a tiled corridor, the walls streaked with pinkish water. The floor was slick. He entered a small, stark room—a foreman’s office. A desk, a chair, a calendar on the wall showing a picture of a pastoral scene utterly alien to this place. He waited.

He did not wait long. The door opened and a man entered. He wore a heavy leather apron, clean, but the ghosts of old stains were etched into it. He was in his fifties, with a broad, placid face and eyes that held no surprise. He carried two tin mugs. He handed one to Jean-Baptiste. It contained hot, bitter coffee laced with brandy.

“Drink. It will warm the wires.”

Jean-Baptiste drank. The heat was a shock, a painful thaw in his core.

The man sat on the edge of the desk. “The next leg is delayed. The contact at the docks was picked up this afternoon. A routine sweep. Bad luck.”

Jean-Baptiste said nothing. He waited for the rest.

“It means you stay here. Tonight. Perhaps tomorrow. We have a room. A safe room. It is not comfortable, but it is secure. This facility processes eight hundred head a day. The police, the inspectors, they come and go. They see only the product. They do not see the walls.”

“And Luc? The man I came with?”

“When he is able, he will be routed a different way. Your paths may converge later. Or not. It is not the concern of this compartment.” The foreman sipped his own coffee. “You are a new problem. We will solve it. It is what we do. We are butchers. We take the whole animal and we break it down into manageable parts. We find a use for every piece. Even the pieces no one wants to see.”

He led Jean-Baptiste out of the office, down another corridor, and to a heavy iron door. He unlocked it with a key from his ring. Inside was a small, windowless room. It held a cot, a bucket, a shelf with a jug of water and a loaf of bread. The walls were white tile, floor to ceiling. It was a clean room, a cold room. It smelled of chlorine and damp cement.

“The latrine is at the end of the hall. Do not go there between six in the morning and ten at night. The day shift uses it. You will be seen. You exist only in the margins of the clock here. Do you understand?”

Jean-Baptiste nodded.

The foreman paused at the door. “You asked the brute who he was. He gave you a poet’s answer. I will give you a butcher’s. We are the ones who handle the mess. The state, it likes its meat packaged, labelled, sterile. It does not like to remember the slaughterhouse. It does not like the smell. So it pretends we do not exist. That is our advantage. We exist in the smell.”

He closed the door. The lock turned with a solid, definitive click.

Jean-Baptiste sat on the cot. The tile room was silent, a vacuum. The only sound was the distant, rhythmic thump of machinery—a hydraulic press, perhaps, or a compressor. The heartbeat of the abattoir.

He was in a white, clean cell in the heart of the stench. He had traded the grey rain for this sterile silence, the open road for this frozen pause. The chain had not broken. It had simply coiled back on itself, holding him in a loop of waiting.

He thought of the ledger. His own name, once entered in the column of reliable functionaries, was now being erased, transferred, rewritten in the secret margins. He was a debt being collected, not by the state, but by its inverse image—a shadowy twin of compartmentalised, grinding efficiency. A twin born of the same class of men: those who worked with their hands, who understood materials, who knew that everything, in the end, could be broken down and processed.

He lay back on the cot. The ceiling was white tile. He stared at the grout lines, the perfect grid. He was inside the grid now, a single cell in a vast, hidden cellular structure. The machinery had remembered the splinter. And now, it seemed, the very material the machinery was built from had its own memory, its own purpose. He had not escaped the system. He had been absorbed by its counterpart.

The hook for the next link was not a person, not a truck. It was this pause. This silence. This cold, clean room where he was to wait, like a side of beef in a cooler, for the next step in the process. The machinery had not righted itself. It had simply opened a different door, and swallowed him whole.

The thump of the distant press continued. Thump. Thump. Thump. A patient, relentless pulse. It was the sound of work. It was the sound of things being broken down into their component parts. He closed his eyes. In the dark behind his lids, he saw the brute’s face in the rain. A man who got tired of moving furniture.

Jean-Baptiste was not tired. He was just beginning to understand the weight of the pieces.



---

Chapter 23

CHAPTER 23

The silence was not an absence. It was a material. It filled the white-tiled cell with the density of packed earth, a sterile loam that pressed against Jean-Baptiste’s eardrums, against his skin. The distant, rhythmic thump of the machinery was not a sound within the silence, but its regulator—the piston driving the compaction. He lay on the cot, a specimen pinned to cork. The grout lines on the ceiling were the edges of his world, a perfect grid of white mortar on white tile. He was inside the ledger now, not a figure in a column, but a smear of ink being blotted, absorbed into the paper’s very fibre.
